# Global, regional, and national burden and trends of multidrug-resistant tuberculosis and extensively drug-resistant tuberculosis in adolescents and adults aged 15-49 years from 2010 to 2021: insights from the global burden of disease study 2021.

</institution></institution-wrap>Chengdu, China </aff></contrib-group><pub-date pub-type="epub"><day>28</day><month>7</month><year>2025</year></pub-date><pub-date content-type="ccbyncndlicense">https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-nd/4.0/</ali:license_ref><license-p><bold>Open Access</bold> This article is licensed under a Creative Commons Attribution-NonCommercial-NoDerivatives 4.0 International License, which permits any non-commercial use, sharing, distribution and reproduction in any medium or format, as long as you give appropriate You do not have permission under this licence to share adapted material derived from this article or parts of it. The images or other third party material in this article are included in the article&#x02019;s Creative Commons licence, unless indicated otherwise in a credit line to the material. If material is not included in the article&#x02019;s Creative Commons licence and your intended use is not permitted by statutory regulation or exceeds the permitted use, you will need to obtain permission directly from the copyright holder. To view a copy of this licence, visit <ext-link ext-link-type="uri" xlink:href="https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-nd/4.0/">http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-nd/4.0/</ext-link>.</license-p></license></permissions><abstract id="Abs1"><sec><title>Background</title><p id="Par4">Multidrug-resistant tuberculosis (MDR-TB) and extensively drug-resistant tuberculosis (XDR-TB) among those aged 15&#x02013;49 years pose a severe public health challenge, yet our understanding of the burden of these diseases in this age group remains limited. This study aimed to evaluate the trends in MDR-TB and XDR-TB burden among this population from 2010 to 2021 across global, regional, and national levels.</p></sec><sec><title>Methods</title><p id="Par5">This study extracted four key indicators&#x02014;incidence, prevalence, deaths, and disability-adjusted life years (DALYs) per 100,000 population&#x02014;for MDR-TB and XDR-TB among 15&#x02013;49 years from the 2021 Global Burden of Disease (GBD) study. It assessed the burden trends using percentage change (PC) and estimated annual percentage change (EAPC), with further analysis by age, sex, and sociodemographic index (SDI).</p></sec><sec><title>Results</title><p id="Par6">In 2021, the global incidence, prevalence, deaths, and DALYs of MDR-TB among adolescents and young adults were 241,399, 336,746, 33,285, and 1,896,002, respectively. Global MDR-TB incidence and DALYs rates showed slight decreases since 2010, with EAPCs of -0.76 and -2.61, respectively. In 2021, the global incidence, prevalence, deaths, and DALYs of XDR-TB among adolescents and young adults were 12,861, 14,039, 2442, and 133,610, respectively. Since 2010, global XDR-TB incidence rates have increased, with an EAPC of 0.57, while prevalence and death rates have decreased, with EAPCs of&#x02009;&#x02212;&#x02009;2.67 and&#x02009;&#x02212;&#x02009;2.87, respectively. The incidence and prevalence rates of MDR-TB were significantly decreased since 2010 in high SDI, high-middle SDI, and low SDI regions. The prevalence rate of XDR-TB was significantly decreased since 2010 in the high SDI and high-middle SDI regions, while a significant increase was observed in the middle SDI, &#x000a0;low-middle SDI, and low SDI regions. Furthermore, a gradual decline was observed in the burden of MDR-TB and XDR-TB as the SDI level increases. The burden of MDR-TB and XDR-TB showed an upward trend during the COVID-19 epidemic.</p></sec><sec><title>Conclusions</title><p id="Par7">The burden of MDR-TB and XDR-TB among adolescents and young adults remained very severe, particularly in the middle SDI and low-middle SDI regions. The COVID-19 pandemic may impact the global burden of these drug-resistant tuberculosis. Targeted interventions are crucial to address this issue.</p></sec><sec><title>Supplementary Information</title><p>The online version contains supplementary material available at 10.1186/s12916-025-04269-7.</p></sec></abstract><kwd-group xml:lang="en"><title>Keywords</title><kwd>DALYs</kwd><kwd>Disease burden</kwd><kwd>GBD 2021</kwd><kwd>MDR-TB</kwd><kwd>XDR-TB</kwd><kwd>SDI</kwd></kwd-group><custom-meta-group><custom-meta><meta-name>issue-copyright-statement</meta-name><meta-value>&#x000a9; BioMed Central Ltd., part of Springer Nature 2025</meta-value></custom-meta></custom-meta-group></article-meta></front><body><sec id="Sec1"><title>Background</title><p id="Par24">Drug-resistant tuberculosis (DR-TB), which includes multidrug-resistant tuberculosis (MDR-TB) and extensively drug-resistant tuberculosis (XDR-TB), is a disease caused by the development of resistance in <italic>Mycobacterium tuberculosis</italic> (Mtb) to anti-tuberculosis drugs. It is estimated to cause 13% of all deaths worldwide due to antimicrobial resistance, posing a significant threat to public health and representing a major public health crisis [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR1">1</xref>]. According to the World Health Organization&#x02019;s Global Tuberculosis Report 2022 [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR2">2</xref>], there were an estimated 450,000 new cases of DR-TB worldwide in 2021, an increase of 3.1% from 437,000 in 2020. A study based on the GBD 2021 study reported that the global incidence of MDR-TB has not experienced a decline since 2015, instead exhibiting a gradual upward trajectory in recent years. Furthermore, there has been a notable escalation in the incidence of XDR-TB. Projections suggest a significant increase in the incidence rates of both MDR-TB and XDR-TB anticipated from 2022 to 2035 [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR3">3</xref>]. Although the global treatment success rate for DR-TB is approximately 60%, in some countries and regions this percentage is even lower [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR2">2</xref>]. A meta-analysis involving 12,030 patients with MDR-TB from 25 countries reported [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR4">4</xref>] that 7346 (61%) achieved treatment success, while 1017 (8%) experienced treatment failure or relapse, and 1729 (14%) died. In 2023, a systematic review and meta-analysis [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR5">5</xref>] of 10,223 patients with XDR-TB from 26 countries revealed a treatment success rate of only 44.2%. In addition, the treatment of DR-TB is characterized by a long duration and unsustainably high costs, imposing a significant economic burden on patients and their families [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR6">6</xref>]. Regions such as Eastern Europe, Africa, the Southern Caucasus, South-East Asia, and Central Asia bear a greater burden of DR-TB. These areas often have lower economic status and treatment adherence, exacerbating the DR-TB epidemic and significantly impacting global DR-TB control efforts [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR7">7</xref>, <xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR8">8</xref>]. Additionally, the COVID-19 pandemic has significantly aggravated the global burden of DR-TB in recent years [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR9">9</xref>]. Against this background, it is particularly important to delve into the global epidemiological situation of MDR-TB and XDR-TB across countries and regions worldwide, to guide the formulation of global strategies for the prevention and control of DR-TB.
</p><p id="Par25">The combined effects of low fertility and high life expectancy have accelerated global population ageing. As the backbone of socioeconomic development, adolescents and adults aged 15&#x02013;49&#x000a0;years are of special importance [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR10">10</xref>, <xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR11">11</xref>]. As they are in their reproductive and prime working years, they experience many physical, emotional, and psychosocial changes during the transition to adulthood. Diseases, career development, higher education, interpersonal relationships, and family formation can all impact these changes [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR11">11</xref>]. Also, this age group is uniquely positioned in DR-TB transmission chains, with potential DR-TB spread in schools, workplaces, etc. Recently, the disease burden of adolescents and adults aged 15&#x02013;49&#x000a0;years, including chronic obstructive pulmonary disease [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR12">12</xref>], early-onset genitourinary cancers [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR13">13</xref>], early-onset pancreatic cancer [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR11">11</xref>], and reproductive health issues [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR14">14</xref>], has drawn global attention. Therefore, focusing on the burden and trends of DR-TB in adolescents and adults hold important value. The GBD 2021 study, an extensive worldwide observational epidemiological study to date, assesses the incidence, prevalence, deaths, and DALYs disability of 371 diseases by age, sex, location, and year [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR15">15</xref>], which presents an opportunity to better understand the epidemiology of HIV-negative MDR-TB and XDR-TB. In this article, we aimed to describe the burden of MDR-TB and XDR-TB, by age, sex, and social development index (SDI), in 204 countries and territories in adolescents and adults aged 15&#x02013;49&#x000a0;years from 2010 to 2021. The availability of disease burden estimates for MDR-TB and XDR-TB would provide a better understanding of the impact of MDR-TB and XDR-TB on the health of adolescents and adults and the need for appropriate prevention strategies and allocation of health resources.</p></sec><sec id="Sec2"><title>Methods</title><sec id="Sec3"><title>Study population and data collection</title><p id="Par26">We obtained data on MDR-TB and XDR-TB from the Global Burden of Diseases, Injuries, and Risk Factors Study (GBD) 2021 using the Global Health Data Exchange (GHDx) results tool. GBD 2021 offers the latest estimates of epidemiological data on the global burden of 371 diseases and injuries across 21 GBD regions and 204 countries and territories from 1990 to 2021 [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR15">15</xref>]. These data are freely accessible from the GHDx (<ext-link ext-link-type="uri" xlink:href="https://ghdx.healthdata.org/gbd-2021/sources">https://ghdx.healthdata.org/gbd-2021/sources</ext-link>), with detailed information on the data, methods, and statistical modeling available in previous reports [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR15">15</xref>, <xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR16">16</xref>]. The GBD 2021 classifies causes into four levels. MDR-TB and XDR-TB were classified as level 4 causes in GBD 2021, which includes pulmonary TB and extrapulmonary TB, whether bacteriologically confirmed or clinically diagnosed [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR17">17</xref>, <xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR18">18</xref>]. MDR-TB is characterized by resistance to isoniazid and rifampicin, while remaining susceptible to all fluoroquinolones and second-line injectable agents. XDR-TB is defined by resistance to isoniazid and rifampicin, as well as to any fluoroquinolone and at least one second-line injectable drug [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR17">17</xref>, <xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR18">18</xref>].</p><p id="Par27">The categorization of TB adheres to the guidelines established by the International Statistical Classification of Diseases (ICD) and Related Health Problems. The relevant ICD-10 codes for HIV-negative TB are A10-A19.9, B90-B90.9, K67.3, K93.0, M49.0, and P37.0. The corresponding ICD-9 codes are 010&#x02013;019.9, 137&#x02013;137.9, 138.0, 138.9, 139.9, 320.4, and 730.4&#x02013;730.6 [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR16">16</xref>]. In this study, we specifically selected adolescents and adults as our study population which ages in the range 15&#x02013;49 years were divided into seven GBD age groups at 5-year intervals. Our aim is to offer targeted strategic recommendations to countries and regions around the world for effectively combating MDR-TB and XDR-TB. To achieve this, we have limited our data collection to the period from 2010 to 2021. In this study, the first author extracted numbers and rates on the incidence, prevalence, deaths, and DALYs of the MDR-TB and XDR-TB within the age range of 15&#x02013;49 years from the GBD 2021 through the GBD Results Tool (<ext-link ext-link-type="uri" xlink:href="https://ghdx.healthdata.org/gbd-results-tool">https://ghdx.healthdata.org/gbd-results-tool</ext-link>).</p><p id="Par28">The Bayesian meta-regression model was mainly used to estimate the incidence and prevalence, while the cause-of-death ensemble model approach was used to estimate the number of deaths and mortality rate. The disability weight quantifies the health loss severity or the degree of nonfatal disability. Years lived with disability (YLD) is calculated by multiplying the number of patients by the time span until recovery or death, along with the disability weight. Years of life lost (YLL) are determined by the product of the mortality rate and the expected lifespan as per a standard life expectancy table. The DALYs encapsulates the aggregate healthy years forfeited from the disease&#x02019;s inception to the individual&#x02019;s demise, representing the aggregate of YLL and YLD [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR19">19</xref>].</p></sec><sec id="Sec4"><title>Sociodemographic index</title><p id="Par29">The sociodemographic index (SDI), an encompassing metric introduced by the Institute for Health Metrics and Evaluation (IHME) in 2015, is intended to evaluate the development level of countries or regions, with a particular focus on the interrelationship between social development and population health outcomes. In summary, the SDI is derived as the geometric mean of a normalized index (ranging from 0 to 1), which integrates the total fertility rate for females under 25 years of age, the average educational attainment (measured in years of schooling) for individuals over 15 years of age, and the lag-distributed per capita income. For the GBD 2021, the SDI values were rescaled by multiplying by 100, thereby creating a range from 0 to 100. Within this scale, a value of 0 represents the lowest levels of income and educational attainment combined with the highest fertility rates, while a value of 100 signifies the highest levels of income and educational attainment along with the lowest fertility rates. The 204 countries and territories were stratified into five quintiles according to country-level estimates of the SDI for the year 2021. These quintiles were categorized as follows: low SDI region (&#x0003c;&#x02009;0.46), low-middle SDI region (0.46&#x02013;0.60), middle SDI region (0.61&#x02013;0.69), high-middle SDI region (0.70&#x02013;0.81), and high SDI region (&#x0003e;&#x02009;0.81) [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR20">20</xref>, <xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR21">21</xref>].</p></sec><sec id="Sec5"><title>Statistical analysis</title><p id="Par30">All rates are expressed per 100,000 individuals annually. The 95% uncertainty intervals (UIs) were used for all estimates in the GBD 2021 database. Uncertainty was propagated through each step of the MDR-TB and XDR-TB estimation process, with UIs representing the 2.5th and 97.5th percentiles of the distribution of 1000 draws. Consequently, when comparing two numerical values (numbers, rates, or percentages), statistical significance could not be directly calculated. If the UIs overlapped, it indicated no significant difference (<italic>P</italic>&#x02009;&#x0003e;&#x02009;0.05). Conversely, if the UIs did not overlap, a statistical difference existed (<italic>P</italic>&#x02009;&#x0003c;&#x02009;0.05). The percentage change (PC) in the number of incidences, prevalences, deaths, and DALYs between the years 2010 and 2021 was determined using the following equation [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR22">22</xref>]: PC&#x02009;=&#x02009;(2021 cases&#x02009;&#x02212;&#x02009;2010 cases)/2010 cases. In previous studies, estimated annual percentage change (EAPC) has been extensively used to track trends in indicators such as prevalence and incidence rates over specific periods of time [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR23">23</xref>]. The estimated annual percentage change (EAPC) of age-specific rates was calculated to estimate the dynamic trends in the rates of prevalence, incidence, deaths, and DALYs of MDR-TB and XDR-TB from 2010 to 2021. It involved a linear regression model <italic>y</italic>&#x02009;=&#x02009;<italic>&#x003b1;</italic>&#x02009;+&#x02009;<italic>&#x003b2;x</italic>&#x02009;+&#x02009;<italic>&#x0025b;</italic>, where <italic>y</italic>&#x02009;=&#x02009;ln (age-specific rate), <italic>x</italic>&#x02009;=&#x02009;calendar year, and <italic>&#x003b5;</italic>&#x02009;=&#x02009;error term. In this formula, <italic>&#x003b2;</italic> represents the positive or negative age-specific rate trends. The EAPC is then calculated as 100&#x02009;&#x000d7;&#x02009;(exp (<italic>&#x003b2;</italic>)&#x02009;&#x02212;&#x02009;1). The EAPC and their 95% confidence intervals (CIs) are used to describe trends over specified time intervals. If the EAPC (95% CI) includes 0, it indicated no significant difference (<italic>P</italic>&#x02009;&#x0003e;&#x02009;0.05). Conversely, if the EAPC (95% CI) does not include 0, a statistical difference existed (<italic>P</italic>&#x02009;&#x0003c;&#x02009;0.05) [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR24">24</xref>]. We used local regression smoothing models (loess) using &#x0201c;geom smooth&#x0201d; function of package &#x0201c;ggplot2&#x0201d; to fit the correlation between the burdens of MDR-TB and XDR-TB and SDI across 21 regions. All statistical analysis and graph plotting were conducted using R software (version 4.2.2).</p></sec></sec><sec id="Sec6"><title>Results</title><sec id="Sec7"><title>The global burden of MDR-TB and XDR-TB</title><p id="Par31">In 2021, 241,399 (95% UI, 137,487 to 419,391) new cases of MDR-TB occurred globally, and the global age-specific incidence rate was 6.114 (95% UI, 3.482 to 10.621) per 100,000. Deaths from MDR-TB were estimated to be 33,285 (95% UI, 13,025 to 67,070) globally, with an age-specific mortality rate of 0.843 (95% UI, 0.330 to 1.699) per 100,000. DALYs of MDR-TB were estimated to be 1,896,002 (95% UI, 770,643 to 3,728,178) globally, with the DALYs rate was 48.017 (95% UI, 19.517 to 94.418) per 100,000. There was no significant change in the incidence, prevalence, deaths, and DALYs case of MDR-TB among adolescents and adults worldwide between 2010 and 2021 (all <italic>P</italic>&#x02009;&#x0003e;&#x02009;0.05, Table&#x000a0;<xref rid="Tab1" ref-type="table">1</xref>). From 2010 to 2021, the EAPCs for MDR-TB incidence and DALYs rate were&#x02009;&#x02212;&#x02009;0.76 (95% CI,&#x02009;&#x02212;&#x02009;1.45 to&#x02009;&#x02212;&#x02009;0.07) and&#x02009;&#x02212;&#x02009;2.61 (95% CI,&#x02009;&#x02212;&#x02009;3.26 to&#x02009;&#x02212;&#x02009;1.96), respectively, while the EAPC for prevalence and death rates showed no significant change (all <italic>P</italic>&#x02009;&#x0003e;&#x02009;0.05; Table&#x000a0;<xref rid="Tab1" ref-type="table">1</xref>; Fig.&#x000a0;<xref rid="Fig1" ref-type="fig">1</xref>a&#x02013;d).

(&#x02212;&#x02009;7.69,&#x02009;&#x02212;&#x02009;1.52)</td></tr></tbody></table><table-wrap-foot><p><italic>Abbreviations:</italic>&#x000a0;<italic>MDR-TB</italic>&#x000a0;Multidrug-resistant tuberculosis without extensive drug resistance, <italic>XDR-TB</italic>&#x000a0;Extensively drug-resistant tuberculosis, <italic>DALYs</italic>&#x000a0;Disability-adjusted life years, <italic>PC</italic>&#x000a0;Percentage change in number of cases from 2010 to 2021, <italic>EAPC</italic>&#x000a0;Estimated annual percentage change, <italic>CI</italic>&#x000a0;Confidence intervals, <italic>UI</italic>&#x000a0;Uncertainty intervals, <italic>SDI</italic>&#x000a0;Sociodemographic index</p></table-wrap-foot></table-wrap><fig id="Fig1"><label>Fig.&#x000a0;1</label><caption><p>The rates of incidence (<bold>a</bold>), prevalence (<bold>b</bold>), deaths (<bold>c</bold>), and DALYs (<bold>d</bold>) in MDR-TB from 2010 to 2021 in global and 5 SDI regions</p></caption><graphic xlink:href="12916_2025_4269_Fig1_HTML" id="MO1"/></fig></p><p id="Par32">Globally, there were 12,861 (99% UI, 8758 to 18,740) new cases of XDR-TB in 2021, and the incidence rate was 0.326 (0.22, 0.475) per 100,000 people. There were 2442 (95% UI, 1075 to 4599) deaths from XDR-TB worldwide in 2021, with a mortality rate of 0.062 (95% UI, 0.027 to 0.116) per 100,000 people aged 15&#x02013;49 years. DALYs of XDR-TB was estimated to be 133,610 (95% UI, 58,772 to 249,288) worldwide in 2021, with the DALYs rate was 3.384 (95% UI, 1.488 to 6.313) per 100,000. There was no significant change in the incidence, prevalence, deaths, and DALYs case of XDR-TB among adolescents and adults worldwide between 2010 and 2021 (all <italic>P</italic>&#x02009;&#x0003e;&#x02009;0.05; Table&#x000a0;<xref rid="Tab2" ref-type="table">2</xref>). From 2010 to 2021, there was an increase in the EAPC for the XDR-TB incidence rate, which was 0.57 (95% CI, 0.19 to 0.97). There was a decrease in the EAPC of deaths and DALYs rates, which were&#x02009;&#x02212;&#x02009;2.67 (95% CI,&#x02009;&#x02212;&#x02009;3.27 to&#x02009;&#x02212;&#x02009;2.05) and&#x02009;&#x02212;&#x02009;2.87 (95% CI,&#x02009;&#x02212;&#x02009;3.25 to&#x02009;&#x02212;&#x02009;2.48), respectively (Table&#x000a0;<xref rid="Tab2" ref-type="table">2</xref>; Fig.&#x000a0;<xref rid="Fig2" ref-type="fig">2</xref>a&#x02013;d).

(&#x02212;&#x02009;8.20,&#x02009;&#x02212;&#x02009;0.99)</td></tr></tbody></table><table-wrap-foot><p><italic>Abbreviations:</italic>
<italic>MDR-TB</italic>&#x000a0;Multidrug-resistant tuberculosis without extensive drug resistance, <italic>XDR-TB</italic>&#x000a0;Extensively drug-resistant tuberculosis, <italic>DALYs</italic>&#x000a0;Disability-adjusted life years, <italic>PC</italic>&#x000a0;Percentage change in number of cases from 2010 to 2021, <italic>EAPC</italic>&#x000a0;Estimated annual percentage change, <italic>CI</italic>&#x000a0;Confidence intervals, <italic>UI</italic>&#x000a0;Uncertainty intervals, <italic>SDI</italic>&#x000a0;Sociodemographic index</p></table-wrap-foot></table-wrap><fig id="Fig2"><label>Fig.&#x000a0;2</label><caption><p>The rates of incidence (<bold>a</bold>), prevalence (<bold>b</bold>), deaths (<bold>c</bold>), and DALYs (<bold>d</bold>) in XDR-TB from 2010 to 2021 in global and 5 SDI regions</p></caption><graphic xlink:href="12916_2025_4269_Fig2_HTML" id="MO2"/></fig></p><p id="Par33">From 2019 to 2021, the global incidence and prevalence of MDR-TB and XDR-TB showed a rebound trend, while the mortality and DALYs exhibited a downward trend (Tables <xref rid="Tab1" ref-type="table">1</xref> and <xref rid="Tab2" ref-type="table">2</xref>; Figs. <xref rid="Fig1" ref-type="fig">1</xref>a&#x02013;d and <xref rid="Fig2" ref-type="fig">2</xref>a&#x02013;d).</p></sec><sec id="Sec8"><title>The 5 SDI region burden of MDR-TB and XDR-TB</title><p id="Par34">There were 1235 (95% UI, 711 to 2335) new cases of MDR-TB occurred in high SDI region in 2021, and the age-specific incidence rate was 0.246 (95% UI, 0.141 to 0.465) per 100,000. However, there were 97,160 (95% UI, 38,722 to 209,332) and 43,816 (95% UI, 24,739 to 76,371) new MDR-TB cases in the low-middle and low SDI regions, which the age-specific incidence rates were 9.561 (95% UI, 3.810 to 20.598) and 8.079 (95% UI, 4.561 to 14.081) per 100,000 people, respectively (Table&#x000a0;<xref rid="Tab1" ref-type="table">1</xref>). The number of MDR-TB deaths in 2021 was only 69 (95% UI, 25 to 160) and 1538 (95% UI, 849 to 2400) in the high and high-middle SDI regions, but 5873 (95% UI, 2013 to 12,041), 16,602 (95% UI, 5147 to 37,177), and 9194 (95% UI, 3861 to 18,660) in the middle, low-middle, and low SDI regions, respectively (Table&#x000a0;<xref rid="Tab1" ref-type="table">1</xref>). The incidence and prevalence rates of MDR-TB were significantly decreased from 2010 to 2021 in high SDI, high-middle SDI, and low SDI regions. However, in middle and low-middle SDI regions, particularly from 2019 to 2021, no significant changes were observed (Table&#x000a0;<xref rid="Tab1" ref-type="table">1</xref>; Fig.&#x000a0;<xref rid="Fig1" ref-type="fig">1</xref>a and b). From 2010 to 2021, the&#x000a0;mortality and DALYs rates for MDR-TB decreased significantly at the higher SDI regional levels (Table&#x000a0;<xref rid="Tab1" ref-type="table">1</xref>; Fig.&#x000a0;<xref rid="Fig1" ref-type="fig">1</xref>c and d).</p><p id="Par35">In 2021, the number of new XDR-TB cases in the high SDI region was 127 (95% UI, 80 to 211), while the number in the high-middle, middle, low-middle, and low SDI regions were 6273 (95% UI, 3954 to 9350), 3017 (95% UI, 1702 to 4990), 2759 (95% UI, 1290 to 5460), and 681 (95% UI, 313 to 1394), respectively. The number of XDR-TB deaths in 2021 was only 13 (95% UI, 6 to 27) in the high SDI region, while the number in the high-middle, middle, low-middle, and low SDI regions were 586 (95% UI, 317 to 929), 580 (95% UI, 237 to 1097), 990 (95% UI, 324 to 2191), and 271 (95% UI, 98 to 584), respectively (Table&#x000a0;<xref rid="Tab2" ref-type="table">2</xref>). From 2010 to 2021, no significant decrease in the incidence of XDR-TB was observed in the high SDI and high-middle SDI regions, but a significant increase was observed in the middle SDI, low-middle SDI, and low SDI regions, with incidence rates of 2.48 (95% UI, 1.56 to 3.40), 3.49 (95% UI, 3.28 to 3.70), and 3.21 (95% UI, 2.99 to 3.42) per 100,000 people, respectively (Table&#x000a0;<xref rid="Tab2" ref-type="table">2</xref>; Fig.&#x000a0;<xref rid="Fig2" ref-type="fig">2</xref>a). From 2010 to 2021, the incidence and prevalence of XDR-TB demonstrated a significant downward trajectory in high SDI and high-middle SDI regions. In contrast, middle SDI, low-middle SDI, and low SDI regions experienced a substantial escalation in XDR-TB incidence and prevalence. Notably, this upward surge was especially evident during the period from 2019 to 2021 (Fig.&#x000a0;<xref rid="Fig2" ref-type="fig">2</xref>a and b). A significant decrease in the rate of XDR-TB deaths was occurred in the high, high-middle, middle, and low-middle SDI regions from 2010 to 2021, while no significant change was occurred in the low SDI region (Fig.&#x000a0;<xref rid="Fig2" ref-type="fig">2</xref>c). There was a significant decrease in the rate of XDR-TB DALYs in the high, high-middle, and middle SDI regions between 2010 and 2021, while there was no significant change in the low-middle and low SDI regions (Fig.&#x000a0;<xref rid="Fig2" ref-type="fig">2</xref>d).</p></sec><sec id="Sec9"><title>The 204 countries burden of MDR-TB and XDR-TB</title><p id="Par36">Figure&#x000a0;<xref rid="Fig3" ref-type="fig">3</xref>a shows the distribution of national-level age-specific incidence rates of MDR-TB in 2021. The 10 countries with the highest age-specific incidence rates of MDR-TB in 2021 were Swaziland, Republic of Moldova, Somalia, Lesotho, Ukraine, Namibia, Russian Federation, Kyrgyzstan, South Africa, and Botswana, respectively (Fig.&#x000a0;<xref rid="Fig3" ref-type="fig">3</xref>a; Additional file <xref rid="MOESM1" ref-type="media">1</xref>, Table S1). China and the USA with the age-specific incidence rates of MDR-TB in 2021 were 1.418 (95% UI, 0.239 to 4.673) and 0.035 (95% UI, 0.011 to 0.095) per 100,000 people, respectively (Fig.&#x000a0;<xref rid="Fig3" ref-type="fig">3</xref>a; Additional file <xref rid="MOESM1" ref-type="media">1</xref>, Table S1). The country with the highest age-specific prevalence rates of MDR-TB in 2021 was Lesotho, with 74.421 (95% UI, 17.325 to 199.878) per 100,000 people (Fig.&#x000a0;<xref rid="Fig3" ref-type="fig">3</xref>b; Additional file <xref rid="MOESM1" ref-type="media">1</xref>, Table S1). The country with the highest age-specific death rates of MDR-TB in 2021 was Lesotho, with 11.001 (95% UI, 2.470 to 31.612) per 100,000 people (Fig.&#x000a0;<xref rid="Fig3" ref-type="fig">3</xref>c; Additional file <xref rid="MOESM1" ref-type="media">1</xref>, Table S1). The country with the highest age-specific DALYs rates of MDR-TB in 2021 was also Lesotho, with 603.915 (95% UI, 137.552 to 1718.437) per 100,000 people (Fig.&#x000a0;<xref rid="Fig3" ref-type="fig">3</xref>d; Additional file <xref rid="MOESM1" ref-type="media">1</xref>, Table S1).<fig id="Fig3"><label>Fig.&#x000a0;3</label><caption><p>The age-specific incidence (<bold>a</bold>), prevalence (<bold>b</bold>), deaths (<bold>c</bold>), and DALYs (<bold>d</bold>) rates of MDR-TB for both sexes in 2021</p></caption><graphic xlink:href="12916_2025_4269_Fig3_HTML" id="MO3"/></fig></p><p id="Par37">The 10 countries with the highest age-specific incidence rates of XDR-TB in 2021 were Republic of Moldova, Ukraine, Russian Federation, Kyrgyzstan, Azerbaijan, Kazakhstan, Georgia, Mongolia, Belarus, and Uzbekistan, respectively (Fig.&#x000a0;<xref rid="Fig4" ref-type="fig">4</xref>a; Additional file <xref rid="MOESM1" ref-type="media">1</xref>, Table S1). The 10 countries with the highest age-specific prevalence rates of XDR-TB in 2021 were Kyrgyzstan, Azerbaijan, Ukraine, Turkmenistan, Uzbekistan, Russian Federation, Belarus, Kazakhstan, and Georgia, respectively (Fig.&#x000a0;<xref rid="Fig4" ref-type="fig">4</xref>b; Additional file <xref rid="MOESM1" ref-type="media">1</xref>, Table S1). The country with the highest age-specific deaths rates of XDR-TB in 2021 was Mongolia, with 1.090 (95% UI, 0.235 to 2.856) per 100,000 people (Fig.&#x000a0;<xref rid="Fig4" ref-type="fig">4</xref>c; Additional file <xref rid="MOESM1" ref-type="media">1</xref>, Table S1). The country with the highest age-specific DALYs rates of XDR-TB in 2021 was also Mongolia, with 57.314 (95% UI, 12.391 to 148.094) per 100,000 people (Fig.&#x000a0;<xref rid="Fig4" ref-type="fig">4</xref>d; Additional file <xref rid="MOESM1" ref-type="media">1</xref>, Table S1).<fig id="Fig4"><label>Fig.&#x000a0;4</label><caption><p>The age-specific incidence (<bold>a</bold>), prevalence (<bold>b</bold>), deaths (<bold>c</bold>), and DALYs (<bold>d</bold>) rates of XDR-TB for both sexes in 2021</p></caption><graphic xlink:href="12916_2025_4269_Fig4_HTML" id="MO4"/></fig></p></sec><sec id="Sec10"><title>Age and sex patterns of global burden of MDR-TB and XDR-TB</title><p id="Par38">The age-specific incidence and prevalence of MDR-TB are lowest in the 15&#x02013;19 age group, but they tend to stabilize for adolescents and adults in age groups above 20. The burden of death and DALYs for MDR-TB increases with age. At same time, the age-specific incidence, prevalence, deaths, and DALYs rates for XDR-TB also increase with age. However, the age-specific incidence, prevalence, deaths, and DALYs rates for MDR-TB and XDR-TB show no differences between males and females across difference age groups in adolescents and adults (all <italic>P</italic>&#x02009;&#x0003e;&#x02009;0.05; Fig.&#x000a0;<xref rid="Fig5" ref-type="fig">5</xref>).<fig id="Fig5"><label>Fig.&#x000a0;5</label><caption><p>Numbers and rates of incidence, prevalence, deaths, and DALYs of MDR-TB and XDR-TB by age group and sex in 2021</p></caption><graphic xlink:href="12916_2025_4269_Fig5_HTML" id="MO5"/></fig></p></sec><sec id="Sec11"><title>The association between DR-TB (MDR-TB and XDR-TB) burden and SDI</title><p id="Par39">In 21 SDI regions, the overall incidence and prevalence rates of MDR-TB in adolescents and adults remain relatively stable when the SDI is below 0.7, but decrease significantly when the SDI is above 7.5. In addition, the death and DALYs rates of MDR-TB are slowly decreasing as the economy improves. Worryingly, an increasing trend in the MDR-TB burden was observed with increasing SDI levels in Southern Sub-Saharan Africa (Fig.&#x000a0;<xref rid="Fig6" ref-type="fig">6</xref>a). As SDI levels increase, XDR-TB incidence, prevalence, death, and DALYs rates among adolescents and adults generally stabilize in most GBD regions. However, an increasing burden of XDR-TB in adolescents and adults is observed in Oceania. In addition, the disease burden in Central Asia and Eastern Europe, while decreasing with higher SDI levels, remains significantly higher than the global average (Fig.&#x000a0;<xref rid="Fig6" ref-type="fig">6</xref>b).<fig id="Fig6"><label>Fig.&#x000a0;6</label><caption><p>The associations between the SDI and incidence, prevalence, deaths, and DALYs rates per 100,000 population of MDR-TB (<bold>a</bold>) and XDR-TB (<bold>b</bold>) across global and 21 GBD regions</p></caption><graphic xlink:href="12916_2025_4269_Fig6_HTML" id="MO6"/></fig></p></sec></sec><sec id="Sec12"><title>Discussion</title><p id="Par40">DR-TB presents significant challenges in treatment, with poor prognosis and high costs. Particularly among adolescents and adults aged 15 to 49, a critical demographic whose reproductive and labor capabilities are vital to societal contributions, the situation remains severe against the backdrop of global low fertility rates and aging populations. This requires our focused attention and proactive measures. In this study, we analyzed GBD 2021 data with a particular focus on age-specific incidence, prevalence, mortality and DALYs rates for HIV-negative MDR-TB and XDR-TB among adolescents and adults aged 15&#x02013;49&#x000a0;years from 2010 to 2021. From a global perspective, the disease burden of MDR-TB and XDR-TB among adolescents and adults remains grim. A 2023 meta-analysis reported global prevalence rates for MDR-TB and XDR-TB of 11.6% (95% CI, 9.1 to 14.5%) and 2.5% (95% CI, 2 to 3%), respectively [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR25">25</xref>]. In our study, the global prevalence of MDR-TB and XDR-TB in 2021 was 8.528 (95% UI, 4.700 to 15.710) and 0.356 (95% UI, 0.234 to 0.543) per 100,000 people in adolescents and adults, respectively. Despite the implementation of various global initiatives aimed at mitigating the burden of MDR-TB and XDR-TB, the persistence of these diseases highlights the ongoing challenges, particularly in the context of the COVID-19 pandemic, increased population mobility, and the escalating spread of drug-resistant strains [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR9">9</xref>, <xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR26">26</xref>]. There is an urgent need for more forward-looking political leadership and sustained financial support to stop the spread of MDR-TB and XDR-TB.</p><p id="Par41">Although DR-TB accounts for less than 10% of TB cases, it consumes more than 50% of national TB budgets [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR27">27</xref>]. DR-TB is a comprehensive test of the economic level of a country and a region, and epidemiologic data show that DR-TB epidemics are particularly severe in regions with relatively backward economies, such as Eastern Europe, Russia, East Asia, South-East Asia, and Sub-Saharan Africa [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR27">27</xref>]. A study reported that patients with MDR-TB accounted for 1.9% of all tuberculosis patients in Papua New Guinea. The treatment of this disease not only affects the employability of patients, but also has a severe impact on family finances, resulting in significant financial losses for 33.9% of patients&#x02019; families [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR28">28</xref>]. Acquired drug resistance may occur during treatment of MDR-TB, leading to the emergence of XDR-TB [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR29">29</xref>]. This situation poses an even greater challenge to TB prevention and control, as drug resistance monitoring is costly, which undoubtedly increases the cost of preventing and controlling drug-resistant TB. Our research shows that from 2010 to 2021, MDR-TB remained at low prevalence levels in high SDI and upper-middle SDI regions, with a declining trend. However, MDR-TB has consistently maintained high prevalence levels in middle SDI, low-middle SDI, and low SDI regions. Meanwhile, for XDR-TB, it remained at a low prevalence level in high SDI and high-middle SDI regions, but in middle SDI, low-middle SDI, and low SDI regions, it not only posed a heavy disease burden but also showed an upward trend year by year. Our research shows that from 2010 to 2021, MDR-TB remained at low prevalence levels in high SDI and upper-middle SDI regions, with a declining trend. However, MDR-TB has consistently maintained high prevalence levels in middle SDI, low-middle SDI, and low SDI regions. Meanwhile, for XDR-TB, it remained at a low prevalence level in high SDI and high-middle SDI regions, but in middle SDI, low-middle SDI, and low SDI regions, it not only posed a heavy disease burden but also showed an upward trend year by year. As SDI levels increase, the global burden of DR-TB has been effectively controlled to some extent in economically developed regions, but it remains severe in economically underdeveloped regions. This further confirms that socioeconomic status plays a key role in the burden of DR-TB. Additionally, other social factors, such as the rational allocation of medical resources, the implementation of public health policies, the effectiveness of public awareness of tuberculosis, education, medical technology, occupation, and social class, remain important factors that cannot be ignored [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR30">30</xref>].</p><p id="Par42">In this study, it was found that males aged 15 to 49 had higher incidence, prevalence, deaths, and DALYs for MDR-TB and XDR-TB compared to females in 2021. This phenomenon may involve multiple factors, including social, economic, and biological aspects. Firstly, from a social behavioral perspective, males in this age group may be more frequently exposed to high-risk environments, such as smoking, alcohol abuse, and occupational exposure to dust or chemical substances, which could weaken their immune systems and increase the risk of infection with <italic>Mycobacterium tuberculosis</italic> and drug-resistant strains [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR31">31</xref>&#x02013;<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR34">34</xref>]. Secondly, economic factors should not be overlooked. In some impoverished regions, men may face malnutrition and bear heavier economic responsibilities. These challenges can lead them to delay seeking medical attention or receive inadequate treatment due to concerns about losing work income when facing health issues, thus missing the optimal timing for controlling the disease [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR35">35</xref>, <xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR36">36</xref>]. Additionally, biological differences may also partially explain this gender disparity [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR37">37</xref>]. Although the specific mechanisms are not yet fully understood, research indicates that differences in sex hormones, immune system responses, and gene expression may affect an individual&#x02019;s susceptibility to <italic>Mycobacterium tuberculosis</italic> and its drug-resistant strains, as well as the progression of the disease and treatment outcomes after infection [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR38">38</xref>&#x02013;<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR40">40</xref>]. It is noteworthy that despite the 15&#x02013;49 age range being a period of strong immune function, the disease burden of MDR-TB and XDR-TB still increases with age. This may imply that over time, cumulative exposure risks, declining immunity, and treatment challenges collectively contribute to this trend [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR40">40</xref>, <xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR41">41</xref>]. Therefore, when formulating policies for the prevention and control of MDR-TB and XDR-TB, it is necessary to consider a comprehensive range of factors, including gender and age issues among patients in different regions, as well as social behaviors, economic conditions, and biological differences, in order to more effectively address the challenges posed by drug-resistant tuberculosis.</p><p id="Par43">There are significant regional differences in the prevalence of MDR-TB and XDR-TB. Studies have reported that the proportion of MDR-TB exceeds 45% in India, China, and Russia [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR42">42</xref>]. A study from China has revealed significant geographic disparities in the burden of DR-TB and the distribution of drug-resistant strains. Among the seven Chinese administrative regions, the highest proportion of MDR isolates was found in Eastern China, accounting for 29.1%, while Northern China had the second-highest proportion at 21.6% [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR43">43</xref>]. Similarly, in the Siberian and Far Eastern Federal Districts of the Russian Federation, the epidemiological situation of DR-TB also shows significant regional differences [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR44">44</xref>]. It is noteworthy that even in the economically developed USA, there are regional differences in the incidence of DR-TB [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR45">45</xref>]. Our research has also found that MDR-TB and XDR-TB show significant differences between countries and regions, with a particularly high burden of disease in countries with lower levels of economic development. These regional differences may be related to local public health policies, availability of medical resources, socioeconomic conditions and population mobility, among other factors.</p><p id="Par44">The global effort to combat MDR-TB and XDR-TB among individuals aged 15&#x02013;49&#x000a0;years has yielded some progress, reflecting the commitment of health organizations and governments worldwide to mitigate the burden of these challenging diseases [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR46">46</xref>]. However, the outbreak of the COVID-19 pandemic has posed challenges to this process, leading to a resurgence in the prevalence of DR-TB. This rebound can be attributed to several factors, including the reallocation of healthcare resources away from tuberculosis control programs, delays in diagnosis and treatment, and disruptions in public health infrastructure [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR9">9</xref>, <xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR46">46</xref>]. The pandemic&#x02019;s impact underscores the fragility of global health systems and the need for resilient strategies to address emerging challenges while maintaining progress against existing threats like MDR-TB and XDR-TB. Future research and interventions should focus on strengthening healthcare systems, improving diagnostic capabilities, and ensuring uninterrupted access to treatment, particularly in high-burden and resource-limited settings. Additionally, a deeper understanding of the interplay between COVID-19 and tuberculosis is essential to inform policies that can effectively address the compounded burden of these diseases.</p><sec id="Sec13"><title>Strengths and limitations of this study</title><p id="Par45">One strength of this study is its focus on the disease burden of MDR-TB and XDR-TB in the 15&#x02013;49 age group, which contributes to a deeper understanding of MDR-TB and XDR-TB in the young population and provides important evidence for early prevention and intervention. However, this study has several limitations that also affect our research. First, the quality of the study may be affected by variations in the quality and availability of GBD 2021 data. Although we employed robust statistical methods to address issues in countries with scarce data, there may be biased estimates of the disease burden due to differences in the quality, comparability, accuracy, and extent of missing data across regions and countries [<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="CR8">8</xref>]. Second, the capacity to diagnose, assess, and treat MDR-TB and XDR-TB is limited, particularly in economically less developed countries, which may limit the representativeness of the statistical data. Third, our study was limited to HIV-negative adolescents and adults aged 15&#x02013;49&#x000a0;years with MDR-TB and XDR-TB, which means that our findings may not be generalizable to all populations. Fourth, some economically underdeveloped countries and regions with a high burden of TB may not be eligible for timely detection of TB resistance, which may result in a subset of patients not being included in the study, further limiting the comprehensiveness of the data. Finally, the GBD database is updated only through 2021, precluding the analysis of more recent global developments. Notably, events such as the COVID-19 pandemic and the military conflict in Ukraine are likely to have influenced the prevalence and dynamics of DR-TB. However, the lack of post-2021 data renders these impacts unquantifiable in the current study. Future research should expand the scope of data collection to include MDR-TB and XDR-TB cases across all age groups, so as to provide scientific evidence and guidance for reducing the burden of MDR-TB and XDR-TB.</p></sec></sec><sec id="Sec14"><title>Conclusions</title><p id="Par46">In this study, the GBD 2021 database has provided a global perspective on the burden of MDR-TB and XDR-TB among adolescents and adults. A significant finding is the continued severity of MDR-TB and XDR-TB in low- and middle-income countries and regions. It is noteworthy that improvements in socioeconomic status may lead to a reduction in the disease burden of these forms of tuberculosis. To reduce the global burden of MDR-TB and XDR-TB among adolescents and adults, decision-makers in countries and regions around the world face challenges that go far beyond ensuring sustained financial support and political leadership. They also need to take into account a range of multidimensional and complex factors, including specific differences between countries and regions; vulnerability of different age groups; gender differences; dynamic changes in sociodemographic structure; the COVID-19 pandemic; and the profound impact of socioeconomic characteristics on disease transmission and access to treatment.</p></sec><sec id="Sec15" sec-type="supplementary-material"><title>Supplementary Information</title><p>

</mixed-citation></citation-alternatives></ref><ref id="CR7"><label>7.</label><citation-alternatives><element-citation id="ec-CR7" publication-type="journal"><person-group person-group-type="author"><name><surname>Dadu</surname><given-names>A</given-names></name><name><surname>Hovhannesyan</surname><given-names>A</given-names></name><name><surname>Ahmedov</surname><given-names>S</given-names></name><name><surname>van der Werf</surname><given-names>MJ</given-names></name><name><surname>Dara</surname><given-names>M</given-names></name></person-group><article-title>Drug-resistant tuberculosis in eastern Europe and central Asia: a time-series analysis of routine surveillance data</article-title><source>Lancet Infect Dis.</source><year>2020</year><volume>20</volume><fpage>250</fpage><lpage>258</lpage><pub-id pub-id-type="doi">10.1016/S1473-3099(19)30568-7</pub-id><pub-id pub-id-type="pmid">31784371</pub-id>
</element-citation><mixed-citation id="mc-CR7" publication-type="journal">Dadu A, Hovhannesyan A, Ahmedov S, van der Werf MJ, Dara M.
